  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_3e5cd4a3-1002-4294-bf9e-11ed49b2642d'

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2321852959715233217">It is 1922, and London is tense. Ex-servicemen are disillusioned; the out-of-work and the hungry are demanding change. And in South London, in a genteel Camberwell villa—a large, silent house now bereft of brothers, husband, and even servants—life is about to be transformed, as impoverished widow Mrs. Wray and her spinster daughter, Frances, are obliged to take in lodgers.</span>
  <span id="freeText2321852959715233217" style="display:none">It is 1922, and London is tense. Ex-servicemen are disillusioned; the out-of-work and the hungry are demanding change. And in South London, in a genteel Camberwell villa—a large, silent house now bereft of brothers, husband, and even servants—life is about to be transformed, as impoverished widow Mrs. Wray and her spinster daughter, Frances, are obliged to take in lodgers.<br /><br />With the arrival of Lilian and Leonard Barber, a modern young couple of the “clerk class,” the routines of the house will be shaken up in unexpected ways. Little do the Wrays know just how profoundly their new tenants will alter the course of Frances’s life—or, as passions mount and frustration gathers, how far-reaching, and how devastating, the disturbances will be.</span>

</div>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/141867-i-don-t-understand-it-says-that-it-will">I don&#39;t understand. It says that it will be published in September 16 and today is the 5th, but there are already reviews down here from a couple of months ago. What is going on?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_141867'><a id="like_count_community_question_141867" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/141867?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F20821087-the-paying-guests">like</a></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/28363483-heather-dawn-stowell"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1391887297i/28363483._UY100_CR24,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/28363483-heather-dawn-stowell">Heather Dawn Stowell</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Some people have access to ARC (Advance Reader's Copy) or something similar. This is when publishers make available copies available ahead of the rele<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Some people have access to ARC (Advance Reader's Copy) or something similar. This is when publishers make available copies available ahead of the release date of their books as part of a PR (public relations) move to promote sales of their books by taking a chance that the book they are offering will garner more good reviews than bad and in doing so will encourage more sales of the book.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_1051686924" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1051686924" />
    <a title="La Petite Américaine Cash App: $Covid2020sucks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59329-la-petite-am-ricaine-cash-app-covid2020sucks"><img alt="La Petite Américaine Cash App: $Covid2020sucks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1218985191p2/59329.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1051686924?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="La Petite Américaine Cash App: $Covid2020sucks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="La Petite Américaine Cash App: $Covid2020sucks" href="/user/show/59329-la-petite-am-ricaine-cash-app-covid2020sucks">La Petite Américaine Cash App: $Covid2020sucks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one. :( </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59329-la-petite-am-ricaine-cash-app-covid2020sucks?shelf=sucked">sucked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59329-la-petite-am-ricaine-cash-app-covid2020sucks?shelf=i-want-my-money-back">i-want-my-money-back</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1051686924" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7307986420129201518">After two years of waiting for Sarah Waters' new novel to come out, reading this actually made me want to cry a little. I don't know what to say. When a book this terrible is written by an author that we know is capable of so much more, it feels like a personal affront. <br/><br/>After a fantastic debut and decades of decent novels, what the hell went so wrong with <i>The Paying Guests</i>? How could our fair Sarah do this to us? <br/><br/>It's all pretty simple. The problem with this book is that Sarah Waters got famous.</span>
  <span id="freeText7307986420129201518" style="display:none">After two years of waiting for Sarah Waters' new novel to come out, reading this actually made me want to cry a little. I don't know what to say. When a book this terrible is written by an author that we know is capable of so much more, it feels like a personal affront. <br /><br />After a fantastic debut and decades of decent novels, what the hell went so wrong with <i>The Paying Guests</i>? How could our fair Sarah do this to us? <br /><br />It's all pretty simple. The problem with this book is that Sarah Waters got famous. Seriously. <br /><br />Think about it. <br /><br />Waters is adored in the literary world, half of her books have been turned into BBC dramas, and she's got more awards up the wazoo than Teen Mom Farrah has glass dongs up the ... nevermind. My point is, Sarah Waters is powerful enough that no one questions her any more. The <i>New York Times</i>, <i>FT</i>, and the <i>Guardian</i> are going to laud her no matter <i>what</i> she writes -- making her clean up the crap isn't worth the trouble.<br /><br />And judging from <i>The Paying Guests</i>, no editor dared email her to let her know she was repeating herself on every goddamn page, or to suggest she rewrite some of the suckier parts.<br /><br />The result is a multitude of cringe-worthy passages:<br /><br />"She seemed to have lost a layer of skin, to be kissing not simply with her lips but with her nerves, her muscles, her blood." (EW). <br /><br />"They smiled at each other across the table, and some sort of shift occurred between them. There was a quickening, a livening – Frances could think of nothing to compare it with save some culinary process. It was like the white of an egg growing pearly in hot water, a milk sauce thickening in the pan." (Huh? How romantic). <br /><br />"It was like being parched, and touching water, like being famished, and holding food." (Sigh. Goddammit). <br /><br />I'm sure some junior editor making 20 grand a year at some London publishing house wasn't about to fire off an email to the great Sarah Waters saying, <br /><br />"Hi S—<br /><br />Just got the feedback from the boys upstairs. <br /><br />Please rework the above-mentioned passages, cut about 30 pages from the melodramatic self-induced abortion scene (it seems all you do is repeat the words "moan" and "pale" and "blood" for several pages), make it harder to see the stupid plot twist coming from 100 pages away, and narrow the last 250 pp. of legal drivel down to 75. <br /><br />Also, can you go for something other than the 1920s English domestic novel? It's duller than deadly nightshade. <br /><br />Finally, please give the two main characters personalities so that readers can tell them apart. Perhaps make them more lifelike--characters that readers can despise, root for, or at least care about--and less like words on the page.<br /><br />Happy to receive your rewrites by Monday at 5. Cheers." <br /><br />Yeah. I didn't think so. <br /><br />Since the people working for her won't say anything, I will. Sarah, you're great when you try. So please try harder. <br /><br />Sorry to do this to you, but...<br /><br />SUCKED.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7307986420129201518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1030044460" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1030044460" />
    <a title="Bonnie Brody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7630898-bonnie-brody"><img alt="Bonnie Brody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571268702p2/7630898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1030044460?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bonnie Brody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bonnie Brody" href="/user/show/7630898-bonnie-brody">Bonnie Brody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1030044460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2505398209588194269">I struggled through this book, and I DO mean struggled. I usually finish a book within three days, even one as lengthy as this one. It took me over a week to read this novel and I did not find it enjoyable. I am new to Sarah Waters though I've had her novel, Fingersmith, recommended to me in the past.<br/><br/>I found The Paying Guests to be overwritten and overwrought. Frances, the protagonist, is given to internal meanderings that repeat themselves over and over again. A good third of this book could ha</span>
  <span id="freeText2505398209588194269" style="display:none">I struggled through this book, and I DO mean struggled. I usually finish a book within three days, even one as lengthy as this one. It took me over a week to read this novel and I did not find it enjoyable. I am new to Sarah Waters though I've had her novel, Fingersmith, recommended to me in the past.<br /><br />I found The Paying Guests to be overwritten and overwrought. Frances, the protagonist, is given to internal meanderings that repeat themselves over and over again. A good third of this book could have been edited out without doing it any harm.<br /><br />Frances Wray lives with her mother in a nice area of London. The setting is just after World War I. Frances's father has died a few years before and has left Frances and her mother in poor economic straits due to carelessness and poor investments. To make matters a bit easier, they take in boarders, or 'paying guests'. These boarders are Lilian and Leonard Barber, both from the 'clerk class', a little less brahmin in character than the Wrays. In London, during those times, this is important.<br /><br />At one time, Frances was an activist and a feminist. She is now a 'spinster' in her twenties, helping her mother with housework and cooking. She had a lover once, a woman named Christina, who she gave up in order to stay with her mother. She is still friends with Christina who is now living with her female lover, Stevie.<br /><br />Frances gradually falls for Lilian Barber and their friendship and burgeoning romance is the subject of the first and second part of the book. The book has three parts and it moves very slowly, without much action, until the end of the second part. I found myself reading and putting the book down over and over again. I picked it up each time with trepidation. Would anything ever happen?<br /><br />Something does happen. However, it happens so late in the book that I was tired of it all. I just wanted it to end. By the time I finished, I felt exhausted from my reading experience. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2505398209588194269" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_818412169" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/818412169" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/818412169?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=free-from-work">free-from-work</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer818412169" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17221908145302702483">this is another stunner from sarah waters, who seems to be back to her full strength after the divisive <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger' title='The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Little Stranger</a>. this book, to me, was nearly as good as the unbeatable <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith' title='Fingersmith by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fingersmith</a>. <br/><br/>this is her first novel set just post-WWI, and it is such a perfect setting for her to be writing in, considering her typical themes of gender and class. WWI was an unprecedented situation for england, with far-reaching and unforeseen consequences affecting those left behind. while the boys were off fighti</span>
  <span id="freeText17221908145302702483" style="display:none">this is another stunner from sarah waters, who seems to be back to her full strength after the divisive <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger" title="The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">The Little Stranger</a>. this book, to me, was nearly as good as the unbeatable <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith" title="Fingersmith by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Fingersmith</a>. <br><br>this is her first novel set just post-WWI, and it is such a perfect setting for her to be writing in, considering her typical themes of gender and class. WWI was an unprecedented situation for england, with far-reaching and unforeseen consequences affecting those left behind. while the boys were off fighting, the women were left behind to pick up the slack, and were granted more employment and social opportunities than they had previously enjoyed. when the men came back, those who managed to survive the fighting, they were frequently unable to find work, and were resentful, suspicious, and frequently even violent towards women who had become almost a different species in their absence. these men didn't fit into the postwar world, they were diminished; their prospects had vanished. the end of the fighting and the return of the men affected the women, too, who had flourished in the relaxation of public scrutiny, in the different demands of a world without men, especially those women who were not looking for a husband to take care of them. <br><br>which brings us to frances. frances is adjusting to the postwar climate as best as she is able. she is unmarried, her father and brothers have died, and she and her mother's social position has altered drastically. her father's mismanagement of their finances has forced them to go without servants, leaving the household chores to frances herself. the possibilities the war offered to women like frances have evaporated, and she is as resentful as the returned soldiers, her prospects just as dried-up, her life reduced to scrubbing the floor on her hands and knees day after day. in order to bring in some money, she and her mother suffer the further humiliation of taking on boarders, or as they are more elegantly-termed, "paying guests," renting out a portion of their overlarge house to a lower-class young couple, lilian and leonard barber. <br><br>the arrival of the barbers is jarring to frances. they are young, modern, and unfettered by the genteel manners to which she is accustomed, even though she herself has had a flirtation with impropriety during the war which landed her both in jail and into the arms of another woman. but now she is on her own, trying desperately to keep up appearances for the sake of her pride and the comfort of her mother, and the barbers are a necessary evil to be endured.<br><br>what follows is a difficult period of adjustment, where the boundaries between private and public spaces blur, the most tense and awkward game of snakes &amp; ladders ever is played, the forced intimacy of the lodgers breaks down several different types of barriers, and then something awful, horrible, terrible occurs - one of the most intensely graphic scenes i have ever read which then paves the way for a series of increasingly-precarious situations compromising all of the characters.<br><br>it's a good thing frances is so good at keeping secrets.<br><br>sarah waters is just so, so good. she has written a deeply romantic, morally complicated book that is a triumph both as historical fiction and as a character-rich psychological suspense thriller. it lacks the mind-bending loyalty-shifts that made <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith" title="Fingersmith by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Fingersmith</a> such an outstandingly original work, but this stands firmly on its own, and is a definite must-read.<br><br>................................................................................................................................................<br><br><br>GIMMIE GIMMIE GIMMIE GIMMIE<br><br>three months later:<br><br>I HAVE IT I HAVE IT I HAVE IT<br><br>ALL MY DREAMS ARE COMING TRUE!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>

<div id="review_994920651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/994920651" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2228181-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507660688p2/2228181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/994920651?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2228181-jill?shelf=psychological-mysteries">psychological-mysteries</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer994920651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4669742310857630421">Up until now, I have not read Sarah Waters. But if any of her previous books are half as compelling and page-turning as The Paying Guest, I have a big treat to look forward to in the near future.<br/><br/>To sum it up in three words: it’s a stunner. Any exploration of the plot will spoil the carefully-constructed narrative with its unexpected twists and turns. The bare bones are these: Frances Wright, considered a spinster at age 27 and believed a little “odd” because she is a lesbian, lives with her newl</span>
  <span id="freeText4669742310857630421" style="display:none">Up until now, I have not read Sarah Waters. But if any of her previous books are half as compelling and page-turning as The Paying Guest, I have a big treat to look forward to in the near future.<br /><br />To sum it up in three words: it’s a stunner. Any exploration of the plot will spoil the carefully-constructed narrative with its unexpected twists and turns. The bare bones are these: Frances Wright, considered a spinster at age 27 and believed a little “odd” because she is a lesbian, lives with her newly-impoverished mother in an upscale south part of London. Due to the ravages of the first world war, she and her mother are forced to accept lodgers (euphemistically called “paying guests”): a young couple, Leonard and Lilian Barber, of the clerk class. There are fractures in the couple’s marriage and soon, the situation will turn dire and fraught with consequences.<br /><br />Why did I love it? First, the book is psychologically gripping. It alternately deals head-on with the thorniest of moral issues, including guilt, shame, and desperate hidden love. Secondly, the depiction of the postwar social landscape is meticulously painted. The class distinctions, the rise of the clerk class, the pretensions of the upper class, the undeserved elitism, are flawlessly rendered. Historically, too, this book is a winner, revealing the dashed hopes of ex-soldiers, the shifting expectations of the various classes, and the mores and manners of the 1920s. <br /><br />Lastly, the careful look at England’s system of justice seems like it's an anachronism in our high-tech world, and that’s what makes it so very fascinating. It's like being permitted a glimpse into a time-capsule…into a society where everything was markedly different than it is today.<br /><br />Exquisitely atmospheric, filled with psychological nuances, and laden with anticipation and suspense, The Paying Guests is a “can’t-put-down” read, told by a consummate storyteller. It is nearly 600 pages and yet I wished it had gone on for twice that long.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4669742310857630421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review994920651">

<div id="review_950740705" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/950740705" />
    <a title="Julie Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine"><img alt="Julie Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/950740705?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=mystery-crime-thriller">mystery-crime-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=best-of-2014">best-of-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=british-isles-theme-setting">british-isles-theme-setting</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer950740705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10452585360375896021">Turn off the phone, unplug the television, call in sick to work. Just do it now, before you open the front cover to <i>The Paying Guests</i>, so you won’t have to tear yourself away later on. <br/><br/>It’s 1922 and life in a dull London suburb has become one of drudgery and tedium for Frances Wray. Single, in her late 20s, and living with her widowed mother, Frances has narrowed her world to a pinpoint of housework and Wednesday trips to the cinema. Her older brothers were killed in the war and her father’s sha</span>
  <span id="freeText10452585360375896021" style="display:none">Turn off the phone, unplug the television, call in sick to work. Just do it now, before you open the front cover to <i>The Paying Guests</i>, so you won’t have to tear yourself away later on. <br /><br />It’s 1922 and life in a dull London suburb has become one of drudgery and tedium for Frances Wray. Single, in her late 20s, and living with her widowed mother, Frances has narrowed her world to a pinpoint of housework and Wednesday trips to the cinema. Her older brothers were killed in the war and her father’s shady financial dealings left her and her mother in ruin, without even a cook or a lady’s maid. A further domestic insult opens the story: the Wrays are forced to take in lodgers, those “paying guests.” <br /><br />Enter stage left: Lilian and Leonard Barber, she blowsy but sweet, he rakish and flirtatious in a way that leaves Frances slightly queasy. The reader, too. The small indignities of sharing a home—the trips through the Wray’s kitchen to access the outdoor toilet; the coughs and blowing noses and creaking of floor overhead, the chance meetings on the landing in one's dressing gown—Sarah Waters chronicles quotidian life in such excruciating detail that we feel as claustrophobic and impinged upon as Frances. But we know we must suffer, for how else will the butcher and gasman be paid at the end of the month?<br /><br />And <i>Voilà</i>: the genius of Sarah Waters. Her scene setting is so exact, so rich and full, the reader is wholly transported to a place and time. She renders the feel of fabrics, the scent of cigarettes and sweat, the cool damp of a garden at night, the weight of a body dragged through the ripe mud, the electric touch of a lover's hand slipping over soft, eager skin. If you have read any other of Waters' lush fiction, you’ll know she sets the bar in the stratosphere when it comes to historical fiction and character depth. No crumpet is left untoasted. No heart left unscathed.<br /><br />But this is so much more than a set piece. <i>The Paying Guests</i> is a crime thriller, to be sure, and something of a courtroom drama in its final chapters; but at its heart, it is the exploration of a woman’s soul. Frances Wray, tucked awkwardly between the end of the Edwardian era and the beginning of the Jazz Age, is stifled by time, culture, and sexual mores that deny her of her very essence. She has already paid a stiff price for a previous romance with a woman; to acknowledge that she has fallen in love again would be to open the door to her final ruin. <br /><br />Yet, Frances is wonderfully, admirably comfortable with her sexuality. It is society that cannot accept her as she is. Ironically, it is this very society Frances allows to strap her down. She could, as her former lover has done, shuffle off to London—only a few tram stops away—and live a far freer life. Yet, she chooses her duty to her mother and manor and remains in a house that "produces dust, as flesh oozes sweat." <br /><br />And oh, those choices. Worse follows bad and the reader sits with her hand pressed to her throat as she breathlessly turns the pages. <br /><br />I remain deliberately oblique about the plot, for any further comments would produce only spoilers. Don’t be intimidated by this tome’s heft. Once started, you will not want to stop. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10452585360375896021" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1162539291" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1162539291" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1232712-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552603488p2/1232712.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1162539291?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/1232712-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1232712-matt?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1162539291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4435123392678754584">Look, I can sit here all day and give you bogus reasons why I picked up Sarah Waters’ <i>The Paying Guests</i>. It’s a lush historical novel. It’s written by a well-respected author known for her twists on that literary genre. It made just about all the top-ten lists of 2014. Yes, I could say all those things, and you might even believe me. <br/><br/>It’s not true, though. And if I can’t be honest with an online book club, mostly filled with people I’ve never met, then honesty doesn’t mean a thing. <br/><br/><i>The Paying Gu</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4435123392678754584" style="display:none">Look, I can sit here all day and give you bogus reasons why I picked up Sarah Waters’ <i>The Paying Guests</i>. It’s a lush historical novel. It’s written by a well-respected author known for her twists on that literary genre. It made just about all the top-ten lists of 2014. Yes, I could say all those things, and you might even believe me. <br /><br />It’s not true, though. And if I can’t be honest with an online book club, mostly filled with people I’ve never met, then honesty doesn’t mean a thing. <br /><br /><i>The Paying Guests</i> caught my eye because of the lesbian sex. There, I said it. I’d never heard of Sarah Waters before. Nothing about the title or cover of <i>The Paying Guests</i> caught my eye or attention. Then, somehow or other, I heard about the plot, and the part of me that never progressed beyond a fourteen year-old boy took notice. <br /><br />So I picked up this book with the vague idea that it was kinky Sapphic erotica burnished with a literary pedigree. <br /><br />Two things: (1) It is decidedly not Sapphic erotica; and (2) It is an awesome book. <br /><br />First things first. Everyone who writes about this book has to, at some point, mention the lesbian angle. It is not merely a curiosity, but the plot driver. Unfortunately, it is also extremely reductive, and does a disservice to Waters’ novel. I realize that as I say this, I come off as a hypocrite, since that’s the whole reason I cracked this book in the first place. But there it is. (They don’t pay me enough to not be hypocritical and internally illogical). <br /><br /><i>The Paying Guests</i> is better described as a love story; and at its core, it is rather traditional, a classic love triangle between a man and two women, with one important twist. (Hint: it involves the two women ignoring the man). One of the characters could realistically be described as a lesbian, but she is never identified with such directness, and Waters is far more concerned with emotional entanglements than sexual identification. <br /><br />That character is Frances Wray. She lives with her widowed mother in a fine old house on Champion Hill in the Camberwell district of London. The year in 1922, and England is still in the aftershocks of the great convulsion of World War I. The Wray family, once part of the British upper class, has fallen on hard times. Two Wray sons have died in the war. Frances’ father is also dead, and has left a legacy of poor investments and debt. <br /><br />In order to keep the Champion Hill house, Frances and her mother have to take in tenants. These are the euphemistically named “paying guests” of the title. Enter Lilian and Leonard Barber. They are a modern, upwardly mobile couple, with Leonard a part of England’s burgeoning professional class. This disconcerts Mrs. Wray, who belongs to the <i>Downton Abbey</i> set, for whom a good name and good breeding are far more important than things like a profession or actual cash-on-hand. <br /><br />Frances is initially unsure how to feel with the new couple in the house. <br /><br /><blockquote>She had done her best to get it all right. But seeing Mrs. Barber going about, taking possession, determining which of her things would go here, which there, she felt oddly redundant – as if she had become her own ghost.</blockquote><br /><br />Later, Frances – who went through a Bohemian phase before the War, which included a girlfriend she still visits – takes a keen interest in Lillian, and the two become friends. <br /><br /><blockquote>How little the two of them knew each other, really. They were practically strangers. She hadn’t had an inkling of Lillian’s existence until six weeks before. Now she’d catch herself thinking of her at all sorts of odd moments, always slightly surprised when she did so, able to follow the thought backward, stage by stage, link by link, this idea having been called to mind by that one, which in turn had been suggested by that… But they all had their finish at Lillian, wherever they started.</blockquote><br /><br />Eventually, Lillian and Frances become lovers. This would be a strained situation in any time – husband, wife, lover, all sharing the same house – but the tension is heightened by taking place in the 1920s. <br /><br />I could say more, but I won’t. <br /><br />Recently, I read Daphne du Maurier’s <i>Rebecca</i>, which turned out to be one of the pleasanter literary experiences I’ve had in awhile. My enjoyment came from everything being a surprise. I didn't know the twists and turns beforehand. <i>Rebecca</i> and <i>The Paying Guests</i> share several similar traits, not the least of which is that I’ve read them both in close relation to each other. Both books give you a twist on the classic love triangle. Both feature houses as a major character. Both start quite slow. And both end extremely fast. <br /><br />I hesitated to recommend <i>Rebecca</i> to others, just as I hesitate to recommend <i>The Paying Guests</i>. It’s not because I don’t like them. To the contrary, I loved them both. But they require a certain kind of reader, with a certain kind of patience. <br /><br /><i>The Paying Guests</i> is a novel about details. The slow, steady accretion of details. For a long time, this is an observational novel, tightly focused on Frances and told from a limited third-person point-of-view. A couple hundred pages go by in which the only set pieces are a trip to a skating rink and a boisterous family dinner. (For those wanting to know about the sex, it comes sometime after page 200). <br /><br />There is something of Dickens in this story. The meandering plot. The wide arc of secondary characters. A creeping sense of aimlessness.<br /><br />At a certain point, the plot clicks into place. Once it does, all the languidness, all the narrative uncertainty, disappears, and everything comes into focus. The book seamlessly transforms into a breathless kind of thriller that has you skimming the words on the page to find out what happens next (which, of course, is a shame, because Waters is a beautiful writer). <br /><br />One of my resolutions for 2015 is a little biblio-financial restraint. I am constantly and impulsively purchasing books, and the pile has grown so high that an entire bookcase is devoted to them. Sometimes I pass that bookcase and marvel at all the time I need and will never get to finish all those books. <br /><br /><i>The Paying Guests</i> was the last thing I read in 2014. It also destroyed my 2015 resolution in its first hours, since I have already bought-out Sarah Waters’ back catalogue. If <i>The Paying Guests</i> is any indication, it will be a good investment, if I ever get around to reading them. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4435123392678754584" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1011387153" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1011387153" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1011387153?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780349004365" href="/book/show/18485452-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1011387153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10706033126364429480">Sarah Waters wrote <i>Fingersmith</i> and so attained semi-divine status in 2002. I really um and ah about reading anything else by one of my 5-star authors because the only way is down. The last two from SW (<i>The Little Stranger</i> & <i>The Night Watch</i>) never reached out tentacled appendages and bade me read them, but this one did.<br/><br/>And I’m in two minds about it, which is almost two whole minds more more than usual. <br/><br/><b>DIALOGUE BETWEEN PB’S TWO MINDS</b><br/><br/><br/>-	What have you been doing for the last 3 days? Reading this 564</span>
  <span id="freeText10706033126364429480" style="display:none">Sarah Waters wrote <i>Fingersmith</i> and so attained semi-divine status in 2002. I really um and ah about reading anything else by one of my 5-star authors because the only way is down. The last two from SW (<i>The Little Stranger</i> &amp; <i>The Night Watch</i>) never reached out tentacled appendages and bade me read them, but this one did.<br /><br />And I’m in two minds about it, which is almost two whole minds more more than usual. <br /><br /><b>DIALOGUE BETWEEN PB’S TWO MINDS</b><br /><br /><br />-	What have you been doing for the last 3 days? Reading this 564-big-pages whopper at great speed. Did you like it as you were hoovering up the plangent prose? Yes! For sure! I know, I was there.<br /><br />-	But, ermm, I thought that, er, maybe, just maybe, there were things that went on a bit too long and other things that didn’t go on long enough.<br /><br />-	Genius literary criticism worthy of David Foster Wallace! Can you expand on that a bit? That was a little too cryptic for me.<br /><br />-	Well, it’s really hard to talk about this novel in ANY kind of detail because it’s 100% about the plot, and the plot is a very simple thing, as a raindrop is simple, until its light refracts through a prism.<br /><br />-	Okay now you’re trying too hard. Give me an example of something which didn’t go on long enough – I mean this is a pretty long novel, yeah?<br /><br />-	The sex. <br /><br />-	Ha, well, now you’re just being sleazy, if you want to read Adult Romances there are other books, and I’m sure some of them are tales of lesbian lust set in the 1920s. No doubt websites too. Try <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.sapphicflappers.com">www.sapphicflappers.com</a><br /><br />-	No but I thought these two lovely characters deserved a bit more of a good time before the Things that I Cannot Mention happened. It just wasn’t fair!<br /><br />-	Only real life is fair, fiction never is.<br /><br />-	And also some of the Things That I Cannot Mention kind of got repeated more than somewhat. And it was all a bit angst. Rather a lot of teeth grinding and snuffling into pocket handkerchiefs. It was a costume drama. White faces behind veils. The accused with bad teeth. <br /><br />-	And yet, little gracehoper, you didn’t stop reading it until the very last page, and I think if there was another 200 pages you’d have read those too, with relish.<br /><br />-	Oh yes, I thought she wrapped it up far too abruptly. It was a bit too much “in one bound Jack was free” or in their case Jacqueline.<br /><br />-	So I’m confused. What do we think?<br /><br />-	Well, this is why we’re in two minds.<br /><br />-	Ah yes.<br /><br />- Hmmm.<br /><br />- Hmmm.<br /><br />-	Four stars then?<br /><br />-	Well, it can’t be five, because of the meagre amount of sex, and because it surely isn’t as good as the shattering Fingersmith. So, yes, four stars it is.<br /><br />The two minds converge. Ka-plunk!<br /></span>

<div id="review_779693162" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/779693162" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/779693162?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

          marked it as dnf-arcs


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780349004365" href="/book/show/18485452-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=womens-prize">womens-prize</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer779693162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11025230580505476428">It's with a heavy heart I have decided I am not going to finish reading this properly. I did, in fact, read more than halfway through before skimming the whole of the rest, right to the end, so really I could claim it as 'read' and give it a rating, but I'm not going to. I was looking forward to reading it so much, and am so disappointed I didn't like it.<br/><br/>Waters' last book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger' title='The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Little Stranger</a>, is one of my favourite books of all time, although the general consensus is that it is quite different </span>
  <span id="freeText11025230580505476428" style="display:none">It's with a heavy heart I have decided I am not going to finish reading this properly. I did, in fact, read more than halfway through before skimming the whole of the rest, right to the end, so really I could claim it as 'read' and give it a rating, but I'm not going to. I was looking forward to reading it so much, and am so disappointed I didn't like it.<br /><br />Waters' last book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6065182.The_Little_Stranger" title="The Little Stranger by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">The Little Stranger</a>, is one of my favourite books of all time, although the general consensus is that it is quite different from the rest of her work, and many readers think it is her worst. I enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/45162.Fingersmith" title="Fingersmith by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Fingersmith</a>, which was truly 'unputdownable' and very emotive, if also quite depressing. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/72929.Affinity" title="Affinity by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">Affinity</a> I found rather dreary (though not without its merits). Of these, I feel <i>The Paying Guests</i> has the most in common with <i>Affinity</i>. It is certainly dreary, and while beautifully written it is incredibly dull, so dull I would have given up much, much sooner had it been the work of an author I was unfamiliar with. The entirety of the first half was boring - mainly building up to a development that every reader will surely anticipate from the first page, so there is no suspense - but I slogged on through, only to be confronted with another twist/'incident' that was also dull and somewhat predictable. <br /><br />I also didn't warm to Frances, the protagonist, at all. I found her a cold, hard character with few sympathetic qualities. At times, I felt I really disliked her, although I couldn't quite put my finger on why - she just seemed like quite an unpleasant person, to me. I found her pursuit of Lilian pushy and manipulative, and I couldn't stop thinking about how horrified I would have been by some of her actions and thoughts had she been a male character. With, for example, Faraday in <i>The Little Stranger</i>, this sort of thing didn't spoil the story because he isn't supposed to be likeable; you're supposed to think he's a creep, that's part of the plot. But I felt I was meant to like Frances, to want her to 'get her way', and I just didn't. I preferred Lilian and felt rather sorry for her, but she never seemed to be fleshed out properly and was only seen through Frances' eyes; Frances' mother was very peripheral; and Leonard was an obvious sleazeball. All in all, not a group of people whose fates I could bring myself to care about.<br /><br />By the time I reached the halfway mark I had a resolute gut feeling that I wasn't going to like the book no matter what; it would have taken something miraculous to change my mind. It even made me question whether I would really like <i>The Little Stranger</i> that much if I re-read it now - maybe my tastes have changed so much I wouldn't? I have, after all, struggled to enjoy any historical fiction in recent times, and have frequently been disappointed with historical novels others have loved, such as the much-fêted debut <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18047651.The_Miniaturist" title="The Miniaturist by Jessie Burton" rel="nofollow noopener">The Miniaturist</a> (which itself was compared to Waters). <br /><br />I'm sorry, Karen. The ARC will be finding its way to someone who will appreciate it more than I did, I promise!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11025230580505476428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1109633389?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B the BookAddict" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B the BookAddict" href="/user/show/19832827-b-the-bookaddict">B the BookAddict</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to B the BookAddict by: </span>
      AAB group members
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19832827-b-the-bookaddict?shelf=hist-fiction">hist-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1109633389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12898669862239090125">I felt a personal link to this story: in 1922, my Nana, Florence, was the same age as the character, Frances Wray. Two brothers, George and Edwin served in WWI and during that war; they made a reciprocal promise to each other. Nana was engaged to George but sadly, he was killed just days before the war ended. Edwin came home to fulfil his part of the promise; he married Florence, who would become my Nana. The death of Florence’s father and two older brothers in the war meant the family money tra</span>
  <span id="freeText12898669862239090125" style="display:none">I felt a personal link to this story: in 1922, my Nana, Florence, was the same age as the character, Frances Wray. Two brothers, George and Edwin served in WWI and during that war; they made a reciprocal promise to each other. Nana was engaged to George but sadly, he was killed just days before the war ended. Edwin came home to fulfil his part of the promise; he married Florence, who would become my Nana. The death of Florence’s father and two older brothers in the war meant the family money transferred to the next living male relative, a cousin. Florence and Edwin kept her four youngest siblings out of an orphanage by electing to bring the children into their home. They raised Florence’s siblings and four of their own children. Another example of how the WWI changed lives for those at home. <br /><br />The review:<br />1922 and England is still recovering from the war, especially in the Wray household. Their reduced circumstances and the loss of the Wray sons and father, have made times tough for the Mrs Wray and Miss Frances Wray of Champion Street. The paying guests, Leonard and Lillian Barber are the new additions to the household. This couple will bring monumental change into the house in Champion Street, in ways neither of the Wrays could have predicted. The friendship between Lillian and Frances begins when they read the novel, Anna Karenina, and discuss their reactions to the novel and it’s characters. A love triangle evolves at Champion Street as the months progress but this is a love triangle, which is surprising to all, both the characters and the reader. As the months pass and the feelings of passion grow, they bring about a climax, which may change all of their fates. I don’t want to give too much of the plot away; it is best discovered by reading the novel.<br /><br />Waters has created an intricate and compound story with esoteric characters. She weaves her plot, building the story seductively; it’s rhythm very much akin to pace of a burgeoning affair, anticipatory, sensory and taut. The story deals with moral issues, postwar England and the realisation of forbidden love; it is a story of both sensual discovery and a psychological drama . Incredibly atmospheric, The Paying Guests is a stunning offering by an already well-established author. 4.5★</span>

<div id="review_1404902563" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1404902563" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1404902563?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780349004600" href="/book/show/24568234-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=historical-fict-20th-cent">historical-fict-20th-cent</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=sexuality-gender-lgbtq">sexuality-gender-lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1404902563" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17214621701917024398"><b>SEX, fingers, blood, fingers, death, gloves, passion, feet, loss, fingers </b><br/><br/>I’m guessing that got your attention. It’s also the gist of the book, though not necessarily in that order. The first half is packed with sexual tension – and release - but most reviews seem to shy away from indicating that. Tastefully explicit, and touchingly erotic; “The PGs” is definitely not PG.<br/><br/>It’s a story of two very different parts (but oddly divided into three sections): the first is a love story, that is doubly ta</span>
  <span id="freeText17214621701917024398" style="display:none"><b>SEX, fingers, blood, fingers, death, gloves, passion, feet, loss, fingers </b><br /><br />I’m guessing that got your attention. It’s also the gist of the book, though not necessarily in that order. The first half is packed with sexual tension – and release - but most reviews seem to shy away from indicating that. Tastefully explicit, and touchingly erotic; “The PGs” is definitely not PG.<br /><br />It’s a story of two very different parts (but oddly divided into three sections): the first is a love story, that is doubly taboo (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[two women; Frances is impoverished gentry, and Lilian is of the “clerk class” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>), and the second is a crime drama, culminating in lengthy court scenes. It’s set in London, in 1922. Frances Wray lives with her widowed mother; the father’s debts necessitate letting rooms to the aspirational insurance clerk, Len Barber, and his somewhat Bohemian wife, Lilian.<br /><br />As I attempt to assemble my thoughts, they won’t really gel: most of the specific points that come to mind are criticisms or more like discussion points for a book group – and yet I eagerly raced through nearly 600 pages (and not just for the sex). That’s more of a mystery than the plot was. What follows may sound like a 3* review; the extra star I’ve awarded it reflects my enjoyment. <br /><br />Overall, it seems a little unpolished, maybe even unfinished. Perhaps Waters has reached the degree of success that, like Rowling, means editors can be ignored, so perhaps they don’t bother to make many suggestions.<br /><br /><b> Sex and Sexuality… Praise and Prejudice</b><br /><br />This is my second Waters. The other was Fingersmith, barely six months ago (review here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/391385777">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>).<br /><br />In Fingersmith, if you know nothing about the author, and especially if you <b>do</b> know the classic novel on which the plot is based, the brief lesbian scenes might have been a surprise. Here, though, it is obvious from the start who the central couple will be. That creates a different dynamic: the titillation of noting all the clues (long fingers, longing fingers, stolen glances, fleeting physical contact, removing gloves, a glimpse of a stocking), waiting for something definite to happen. When it does, it’s far headier than anything in Fingersmith, and fingers feature even more prominently throughout, not just in a direct, sensual way: Len has manicures (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[his mistress is a beautician <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>), fingerprints are used in the investigation, and the significance of rings and removing or putting on gloves is never overlooked.<br /><br />Waters based the book on high-profile murder cases of the period, but gave it a twist, by making Frances female. That gives Frances more of a backstory, and it makes later events more plausible, because most of the other characters never consider the possibility of two women being romantically involved.<br /><br />One specific scene where her gender affects one’s interpretation is in the pantry. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I was uneasy about how consensual that first encounter was: how coercive was Frances being? As the scene developed, those concerns subsided, and that was reinforced by subsequent events. Nevertheless, had Frances been a man, my unease would have been greater. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Does that make me guilty of narrow-minded (hetero)sexism?<br /><br />Another way Waters inverts traditional storytelling is that everyone starts off unhappily… and everyone ends up unhappily!<br /><br />This is not a bad book – I enjoyed reading it and have given it 4* - but I don’t see why it has earned so much praise and so many literary awards. If Francis (male) rented out rooms, would the book have been lost in a sea of similar historical fiction? Quite possibly, but I suppose that’s the point: literature should break free of its relentlessly heterosexual norms, regardless of the sexuality of likely readers. I applaud that, really, I do. But it still doesn’t make this a great book, just an interesting and enjoyable one.<br /><br /><b>Tabloid Press</b><br /><br />The evils of the tabloids are made painfully and plausibly clear: the way they exploit and harass those suffering, and the way gossip sells, even to those who disapprove, but find it irresistible. The press doorstep relatives, drip-feed salacious titbits, crop photos to twist the story, gate-crash a funeral, and so on. This is one of the stronger aspects of the book: it speaks to contemporary concerns without being heavy-handed, while being (I think) historically accurate. Also, it’s a theme that once it arises, is a consistent undercurrent.<br /><br /><b>Ambiguity is Good</b><br /><br />I like the fact there are important, unanswered questions. I’m still pondering these half dozen:<br /><br />1.	<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Who was pulling whose strings? Was Frances the predatory one who ended up with more than she bargained for, or was Lilian using her all along? I think probably both: when Frances first told Lilian she was a lesbian, the friendship cooled. Maybe Lilian rekindled it only because she’d realised how she could use Frances to escape her marriage.<br /><br />2.	Lilian certainly wanted to get out of her marriage, though her motivation is cloudier: was it the lost child, Len’s affair, an entirely separate wish for independence? What was the nature of their relationship, especially the drunken game of Snakes and Ladders, where “Frances had the sense that their antics were a weird kind of show, done for here but not flattering to her”?<br /><br />3.	Lilian had explicitly wished Len dead at least once, and in the heat of the fight, I think she probably meant to kill him. Frances later asks if it was an accident, but Lilian evades the question and “straightens the cuffs of her gloves”. I’m less sure whether she had planned to kill him, or to find some other escape, such as exposing his affair. The life insurance suggests the former, though.<br /><br />4.	I doubt Lilian and Frances grow old together, but I'm less sure how long they might have tried to make a go of it or what sort of relationships either would go on to have. Lilian wouldn’t have stayed single for long, but male or female partner? I fear Frances would just retreat to resentful spinsterhood, either in the house of unhappy memories, with another set of paying guests, or, perhaps worse, in a smaller “clerk class” house with no memories.<br /><br />5.	How much did Frances’ mother guess? She knew her daughter had had one relationship with a woman (Christina) and had been concerned at the intensity of her friendship with Lilian (though that was partly a matter of their different social positions). There were oddities around the time of the murder, some of which she noticed; she even asks Frances outright if she is keeping secrets about the death. If she believed her daughter complicit in murder, how would that affect their already prickly relationship? As an upstanding member of the parish, would she try to persuade Frances to confess (probably), and if that failed, would she report Frances to the police (probably not)? <br /><br />6.	After Spencer’s acquittal, Frances and Lilian are understandably relieved. They discuss what’s happened, the power of love, the pain of an unhappy relationship, all the people who’ve been affected, but they don’t seem overly burdened about having got away with murder/manslaughter or about future police enquiries. Will they be able to shake it off and live almost as if nothing happened (and if so, what does that say about them?), or will fear and guilt gnaw away at them? Frances wonders if finding happiness would be an insult to those who’ve been harmed, or whether that means it’s their duty to strive for it. We don’t know what Lilian thinks. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Lost Plots</b><br /><br />The two main parts of the story hold together well, and I like loose ends. However, there are sub plots and themes that arise, seem important, but are then overlooked, even when they are pertinent. That’s part of what I meant about it feeling unpolished:<br /><br />1.	<b>Prejudice</b> is inherent in the plot; it’s the root of everything, but it felt as if having made it a lesbian story, with an ex-suffragette… that was it. I’m not sure how I would have preferred it, but it seemed as if there was a message struggling to get out, that didn’t. The fact that Frances and Lilian are increasingly unsympathetic characters exacerbates that.<br /><br />2.	<b>Politics</b> is strong early on. We're told about Frances’ activism for women’s rights, to the fury and embarrassment of her parents. After multiple losses (two brothers, her father, and her lover), Frances loses that zeal for protest, which is understandable. However, it seems odd that political ideas cease to be mentioned, apart from frequent reminders that Frances once threw a shoe at an MP (I'm just surprised it wasn't a glove), along with regular crass observations about class differences.<br /><br />3.	<b>Anna Karenina</b> is a book loved by Frances and Lilian, which they discuss early on but then not mentioned for hundreds of pages, until a passing reference near the end. I thought it was going to be a running thread, with parallels to Waters’ story, and perhaps it is (especially the guilt and torment of the second part), but if so, it was a little too subtle for me, and perhaps for a mass-market novel. Either give little hints to the similarities throughout, or (as with Fingersmith) don’t mention the inspiration at all: leave it as a treat for those who know and notice.<br /><br /><b>Convenient Coincidences</b><br /><br />One or two convenient coincidences would be fine, but there were too many significant ones. I don’t read much crime fiction, but surely part of the point is that it’s tightly plotted?<br /><br />1.	<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[A failed marriage is never the fault of just one partner, but Frances and Lilian choose not to see any fault in Lilian at all. All the blame is laid on the pain of the lost child, and on Len. The fact he was always a bit of a letch and then had an affair makes his death less of a tragedy, and so mitigates any guilt.<br /><br />2.	No one saw Len come home up the front path on the evening of his death, even though it was still light.<br /><br />3.	The timing of unrelated events meant Lilian's abortion was assumed to be a miscarriage caused by the shock of Len’s death.<br /><br />4.	Spencer’s admitted previous assault on and threats to Len made the murder charge plausible. The fact Len hadn’t reported it to the police (though he told Lilian and the Wrays he had) made it even more so. The police and prosecution convinced themselves of it and then interpreted the evidence to fit.<br /><br />5.	Spencer's criminal record and “smirking nonchalance” in court made him an easy scapegoat in practical terms, and minimised the injustice of his being remanded and tried.<br /><br />6.	A last-minute witness got Spencer off the hook and off the noose, by confirming his alibi, and thus Frances and Lilian avoided the need to confess to spare his life. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Clunky Metaphors</b><br /><br />I found it a racy read in more ways than one. Waters possibly overdid the fingers/gloves (plus a few bare feet) a little, and there were one or two phrases and metaphors that jarred. They are spoilered not because they give away plot, but because it looks a bit too snarky if they’re not hidden.<br /><br />•	<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">["flurries of wind" – snow may be a cliché, but wind just sounds odd.<br /><br />•	“a striped pyjama suit” – even Americans didn’t/don’t say “pyjama/pajama <b>suit</b>” (I checked the Google Ngrams corpus!)<br /><br />•	"she felt as alive as a piece of radium" – huh?<br /><br />•	"chased away like a cockerel chasing away a ghost" – as if that’s a thing.<br /><br />•	"boneless with exhaustion" – if I’m exhausted, my bones feel heavy, but other friends have identified with this, so it may be unfair to include it here.<br /><br />•	Lilian’s lively décor “was as if a giant mouth had sucked a bag of boiled sweets and then given the house a lick” - yuk.<br /><br />•	The second sentence of the novel, relating to the imminent arrival of the paying guests and surely the book itself is the clichéd “It was like waiting to begin a journey”. But it is contradicted and reworked on the next page, “It was like ending one and not wanting to get out of the train”.<br /><br />•	"There was a quickening, a livening – Frances could think of nothing to compare it with save some culinary process. It was like the white of an egg growing pearly in hot water, a milk sauce thickening in the pan." Cos custard is so sexy.<br /><br />•	Awaiting the verdict, she was “slack as worn elastic yet had the taughtness of wire” – oxymorons can work, but I’m not sure that one does. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Quotes I Did Like</b><br /><br />I thought the gradual build up of tension and passion was really well done, but those lines are all about context, rather than the words themselves. These lines stand on their own, though:<br /><br />•	Furniture moved to a lesser setting seemed “to be sitting as tensely as unhappy visitors… pining for their grooves and smooches in the room above”.<br /><br />•	On Lilian’s eclectic décor, “If only she would decide on a country and have done with it” is almost worthy of Oscar Wilde.<br /><br />•	“Her gaze… seemed always to be in the process of sliding away, and her pose was a cautious one, as if she were reaching into a thicket, trying to avoid being snagged by thorns.”<br /><br />•	After a makeover, “She felt half disguised by the outfit; half exposed by it”.<br /><br />•	“They could never have looked at each other so nakedly in the dangerous privacy of Champion Hill.”<br /><br />•	Lovers escaping a party “feeling unmoored, suspended, lapped about by the liquid blue night”.<br /><br />•	“They went in and out of lamplight, their shadows fluid under their feet.”<br /><br />•	“The muted tap of her wedding-band, a small chill sound in the darkness.”<br /><br />•	“Sunday, that dull, dull tyrant.”<br /><br />•	“The whole furtive business… of finding and securing and making the most of scraps of time with her – those juicy but elusive morsels of time, that had to be eased like winkles out of their shells, then gobbled down with an eye on the door, an ear to the stair, never comfortably savoured – it had all… been crushing the life out of her.”<br /><br />•	Of a police inspector, “Frances had the impression that his friendliness was all surface – or worse than that, was somehow strategic”.<br /><br />•	“Her own fingers felt blind… The ease and familiarity were gone.”<br /><br />•	“<b>Gardening was simply open-air housework</b>.”<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17214621701917024398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/998162100?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dianne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dianne" href="/user/show/2947323-dianne">Dianne</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer998162100" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16785492717941267587">There is so much to like about this book. Sarah Waters slowly and exquisitely sets her scene, 1922 London after World War I. Frances Wray and her mother live in an aging home in a genteel London neighborhood. Their fortunes have suffered due to unfortunate investments and they are forced to take in boarders, called “paying guests.” The boarders are a young married couple, Lilian and Leonard Barber. This simple act of economy has unintended consequences that transforms lives forever.<br/><br/>I don’t want </span>
  <span id="freeText16785492717941267587" style="display:none">There is so much to like about this book. Sarah Waters slowly and exquisitely sets her scene, 1922 London after World War I. Frances Wray and her mother live in an aging home in a genteel London neighborhood. Their fortunes have suffered due to unfortunate investments and they are forced to take in boarders, called “paying guests.” The boarders are a young married couple, Lilian and Leonard Barber. This simple act of economy has unintended consequences that transforms lives forever.<br /><br />I don’t want to give too much away by revealing the details of the plot – I will say that what started out as a lovely piece of historical fiction turned into something else entirely. It became a bit of an erotic gothic potboiler, although with a modern twist, and then a mystery and suspense novel. Until the very end, I was not sure how it would end. <br /><br />Some thoughts – the book is beautifully written. If you enjoy language and prose and leisurely and nuanced writing, you will certainly like Sarah Waters. She does a superb job of creating atmosphere and the milieu of post-World War I London. I do feel it was overlong (it seems like I am always saying this lately, so perhaps this is just a bias of mine). It lagged in the middle third of the book – it got repetitive, I felt like Frances and Lilian kept having the same conversations over and over. The last third was suspenseful but felt artificial. Waters kept slipping in red herrings to misdirect the reader, but it felt forced. None of it seemed very likely to me, especially the neatly wrapped-up ending.<br /><br />This is a 3.5 for me. Full of promise at the start but ultimately misses the mark at the end.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16785492717941267587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>
