<span id="freeTextContainer16218645488939228368">Geepers, this book.<br/><br/>It's just ... oh, it is a boring mess. <i>Fingersmith</i> and <i>Tipping the Velvet</i> and even <i>The Night Watch</i> which I didn't really like all that much at least had things happening that didn't always involve the main characters. The world was moving.<br/><br/>Here, the world is stuck: it's just after WWI and no one really seems to know where to go or what to do.<br/><br/>And for the first half of the book, nothing happens. A couple moves in to Frances' house. That's it. OK, there's some woman-on-woman actio</span>
  <span id="freeText16218645488939228368" style="display:none">Geepers, this book.<br /><br />It's just ... oh, it is a boring mess. <i>Fingersmith</i> and <i>Tipping the Velvet</i> and even <i>The Night Watch</i> which I didn't really like all that much at least had things happening that didn't always involve the main characters. The world was moving.<br /><br />Here, the world is stuck: it's just after WWI and no one really seems to know where to go or what to do.<br /><br />And for the first half of the book, nothing happens. A couple moves in to Frances' house. That's it. OK, there's some woman-on-woman action finding its way in at that point, but it's a Sarah Waters book. That's what happens. That's not really what everything is about, though, because people have jobs and future plans and-<br /><br />Well, no. Not here. There are some dumb plans, but they are not particularly future plans because it is obvious they will never happen. No one really has a job (except for Lenny, but Lenny isn't a character as much as some negative space around which to frame the rest of the narrative - there's no point to the weird menace he has at the beginning of the book, no point to the game of strip Snakes and Ladders). <br /><br />It's just moping, moping, moping, sex, moping, sex, whoops accidental death, moping, drama, drama, drama, fizzle. (The drama isn't even really affecting, as it's 3/4ths in Frances' head anyway.)<br /><br />I was bored. I am disappoint.</span>

<div id="review_1040018611" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1040018611" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1040018611?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1040018611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2405317026642755254">I am conflicted about this novel. The prose is lovely and I really liked the first half of the book, but I grew impatient with the stale plotting of the second half. <br/><br/>The heroine of the story is Frances Wray, who lives with her widowed mother in a genteel home in 1920s London. To help pay the bills, the Wrays take in lodgers, who are politely called "paying guests." The lodgers are a young married couple, Lilian and Leonard Barber. Frances becomes friends with Lilian, and then falls in love with </span>
  <span id="freeText2405317026642755254" style="display:none">I am conflicted about this novel. The prose is lovely and I really liked the first half of the book, but I grew impatient with the stale plotting of the second half. <br /><br />The heroine of the story is Frances Wray, who lives with her widowed mother in a genteel home in 1920s London. To help pay the bills, the Wrays take in lodgers, who are politely called "paying guests." The lodgers are a young married couple, Lilian and Leonard Barber. Frances becomes friends with Lilian, and then falls in love with her.<br /><br />Frances shares her sad tale with Lilian, which is that previously she had a love affair with a woman named Christina, but Frances ended it when her mother found out and became upset. Lilian and Frances begin a secret love affair, and Frances hopes that Lilian will leave her husband and then they can live together. But their plans are interrupted when Leonard surprises them one night. To discuss some of my complaints about the rest of the novel, I need to write a long spoiler.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />As a character, Frances was irritating. Midway through the book, Lilian became pregnant with Leonard's baby and decides she wants to get rid of it. Frances, despite earlier stories of her progressiveness and feminist ideals, objects to Lilian's plan and tries to talk her out of it. Huh? The author doesn't explain why Frances wanted her to keep the baby, especially since Lilian said she wanted to leave her husband. At no point does France say she wants to raise the baby with Lilian — she just suddenly became conservative and objected to the planned abortion.<br /><br />But my real irritation was with the second half of the book. Leonard comes home early one night and catches the women in a suspicious act. Leonard starts bullying Lilian, and she announces that she wants a divorce because she is in love with Frances. Leonard becomes violent, and in an act of defense, Lilian hits him in the head with a metal ashtray, killing him. Frances wants to call the police, but Lilian wants to move the body outside to make it look like he fell in the road. <br /><br />The rest of the book follows a long and tedious police investigation, and then a long and tedious trial when someone else is accused of the murder. Frances dithers constantly, fretting over whether or not they should confess and constantly running to Lilian and whispering about the case. The plot device of an accidental death and trying to hide information from police is so overused that I actually groaned when I reached that point in the book. There was also the trope that Frances became suspicious of Lilian, fretting that she would go to the police and try to pin the murder on her, or that she would break down and admit to the accidental killing during one of those times when Frances didn't want to be discovered. (Frances was constantly dithering between feeling guilty and wanting freedom.) Additionally, the ending of the book was anti-climatic, and I don't think we needed to plod through such an extended courtroom drama to get there.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />In summary, the character of Frances became so annoying that I stopped rooting for her. However, I have to admit that Sarah Waters' prose is lovely. Her descriptions could be beautiful and thoughtful, and I think this is a case in which the novel needed more editing and streamlining. At more than 550 pages, some chunks could have been cut.<br /><br />I wrestled with what rating to give this book, and I think three stars is fair. I did like parts of it, but I would recommend it with caution.<br /><br /><b>Audiobook Note</b><br />I listened to this on audio, read by Juliet Stevenson, and she was fantastic. If I could rate just Juliet's performance, it would be five stars. I loved her reading so much I am going to look up other audiobooks she has recorded, which is apparently an extensive collection. <br /><br /><b>Favorite Quotes</b><br />"Frances didn't like surprises. She hated the thought of people plotting and planning on her behalf. She loathed the burden of being delighted once the surprise was disclosed."<br /><br />"What was the use of her being alive? Her heart was some desiccated thing: a prune, a fossil, a piece of clinker. Her mouth might as well be filled with ashes. It was all utterly hopeless and futile..."<br /><br />"They had no idea how decency, loyalty, courage, how it all shriveled away when one was frightened."</span>
  <a data-text-id="2405317026642755254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1071319186" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1071319186" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2679341-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442635072p2/2679341.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1071319186?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/2679341-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2679341-jane?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1071319186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1847938848131413896">Where I got the book: purchased on Kindle. A read for my IRL book club.<br/><br/>I came to this novel having barely skimmed the description, so I had absolutely no idea what to expect. As I started it, one book club friend was lamenting that she was waiting for something to happen…<br/><br/>And I must admit, this story has a slow lead-in. It’s 1922; London is still recovering from the War, with unemployed ex-servicemen everywhere, many families still mourning the men they’ve lost, and the former servant class deser</span>
  <span id="freeText1847938848131413896" style="display:none">Where I got the book: purchased on Kindle. A read for my IRL book club.<br /><br />I came to this novel having barely skimmed the description, so I had absolutely no idea what to expect. As I started it, one book club friend was lamenting that she was waiting for something to happen…<br /><br />And I must admit, this story has a slow lead-in. It’s 1922; London is still recovering from the War, with unemployed ex-servicemen everywhere, many families still mourning the men they’ve lost, and the former servant class deserting their pre-War positions for greener pastures. Frances and her mother are forced, by their dwindling income, to let out part of their house to lodgers, or “paying guests” as it’s known in their genteel part of London. (I believe Champion Hill is an invented location, but Waters makes it completely convincing.) Their guests turn out to be Len and Lil Barber, several steps below the Wrays on the social scale but definitely climbing—Len has a good job, and Lil leads the idle, pointless life of a lower-middle-class wife. The upper middle classes and those above them are expected to do charity work, and Mrs. Wray still clings to this routine, leaving Frances to do all the housework since they can no longer afford servants.<br /><br />The story gradually builds through the inevitable encounters between the Wrays and the Barbers, mostly due to the fact that you have to pass through the kitchen to get to the toilet. One of the frequently encountered peculiarities of English houses, even less than a century ago, was that even a plumbed-in toilet was outdoors—my parents’ house, which was built in the Thirties, still has theirs, attached to the kitchen wall but very definitely outside (the one in the bathroom was put there later). At my great-grandmother’s house, you had to “go down the garden” to “see Auntie.” I know, it makes me feel like a relic from another age. (My other grandparents’ house had an inside loo of the enormous Victorian variety, with a stout wooden seat, hard semi-transparent paper, and tooth powder instead of toothpaste in the bathroom. I am OLD.) <br /><br />Another location for encounters is the upstairs landing, since Frances still has her bedroom on that floor; the Barbers spill out of their space into the Wrays’, a perpetual intrusion that announces just how much space they are going to take up in the lives of the two Wray women.<br /><br />Despite Mrs. Wray’s reluctance to have anything more to do with “that class of people” than they have to, a friendship slowly builds between Lil and Frances. Along the way we also find out that the great love of Frances’ life was a woman, Chrissy, whom she still visits, but that when it came to choosing between her mother and respectability or Chrissy, social ostracism, and a bohemian lifestyle, Frances took the easier path. Chrissy has moved on, while Frances is still stuck, clinging to the shreds of an old life which is literally falling into pieces around her (her clothing, her hidden underwear in particular, is wearing thin and falling into holes—a nice touch.)<br /><br />And up to this point, despite the fact that there wasn’t much happening, I was absorbed in the story. I loved the depiction of post-War London with its gradually changing social attitudes and the rise of the new type of middle class represented by Lil and Len. I loved the tension the Wray women experience when it comes to keeping up appearances. I enjoy literary novels which present the reader with slow revelation of the truths and lies of the past. I had only a vague idea of where the story was going, but I was comfortable with it.<br /><br />And THEN…Well, I’m not going to tell you. Seriously, this novel is best read without spoilers of any kind—let me just say that my friend’s complaint about nothing happening simply meant she hadn’t read far enough into the book. There were several twists, too, so just when I thought I was pretty sure of the outcome, the rug was pulled out from under my feet and I had to start guessing all over again. Which is why I stayed up way too late last night so I could finish it.<br /><br />The writing is beautiful, without a single word out of place or any slip into too-modern speech or attitudes. It’s a novel in the tradition of literary realism, a very warts-and-all look at humankind that nonetheless never slides into outright pessimism or dislike of the human race. I’m trying to decide whether Waters wrote from a stance of moral impartiality (as far as that can be achieved) or whether her sympathies did, after all, lean toward her protagonists. She does make it clear that we have a choice as to the paths we are going to take, even when we feel caught up in events, but shows, I think, that we make those choices wearing a blindfold.<br /><br />Some of the realism overwhelmed me at times, especially at one point when I felt there were far too many bodily fluids sloshing around. I have my limits. And there was something eerily symbolic about that whole business of constantly passing through the kitchen to get to the bathroom…and the house that Frances never seemed able to keep clean after a while, and that began literally disintegrating by the end. Of course it was a whole world that disintegrated as a result of the First World War, only I don’t suppose the people caught up in that war actually realized it until much later.<br /><br />All ramblings aside, this was a nice literary read, and a painstakingly researched piece of historical fiction to boot. I feel like there are some very strong writers in the British Isles right now, many of them of an age that promises a good many books to come. Going to Sarah Waters’ author page, I note I have three more of her books on my TBR list—I just requested <i>The Little Stranger</i> from the library, to round out my acquaintance with her writing.<br /><br />I think <i>The Paying Guests</i> will probably earn a place in my Top Ten for 2014. Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1847938848131413896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1111641931" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1111641931" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598060221p2/2172096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1111641931?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2172096-barbara?shelf=wom-2015-challenge">wom-2015-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1111641931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5506423230207033068"><br/><b>3.5 stars</b><br/><br/>Formerly well-off Frances Wray and her mother are having a hard time in post-WWI London. Frances' brothers were lost in the war and her father died leaving a load of debt. To conserve money the Wrays had to let the help go. So 26-year-old Frances has to do all the household chores..... <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596292976i/29899565.jpg' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>.....while her mother - apparently unable or unwilling to do any cooking or cleaning - goes about her personal business. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596294366i/29899666.png' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Thus Frances is generally roughly dressed, tired, and sporting the rough, red hands</span>
  <span id="freeText5506423230207033068" style="display:none"><br><b>3.5 stars</b><br><br>Formerly well-off Frances Wray and her mother are having a hard time in post-WWI London. Frances' brothers were lost in the war and her father died leaving a load of debt. To conserve money the Wrays had to let the help go. So 26-year-old Frances has to do all the household chores..... <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596292976i/29899565.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>.....while her mother - apparently unable or unwilling to do any cooking or cleaning - goes about her personal business. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596294366i/29899666.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Thus Frances is generally roughly dressed, tired, and sporting the rough, red hands of a charwoman. Not the life she had pictured for herself.<br><br>To help pay the bills the Wrays decide to rent part of their house to paying guests, Leonard and Lillian Barber. The Barbers are an upwardly mobile twenty-something couple that have little in common with the Wrays. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596292976i/29899564._SX540_.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Leonard works for an insurance company while Lillian stays home lounging and decorating her rooms to resemble an exotic bazaar. <br><br>Though Frances is put off by brash, intrusive Leonard she starts to becomes friendly with the Barbers. On a night when there's been too much drinking this leads to an uncomfortable game of 'Snakes and Ladders' where a drunk Leonard enforces his own made-up rules. Frances is embarrassed and starts to sense some trouble in the Barber marriage.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596292976i/29899563.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Living in the same house, Frances and Lillian become friendly, with chats and walks and picnics. Eventually Lillian cuts and waves Frances' hair, updates her party gown, and takes her to a relative's birthday party.....<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596292976i/29899567.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>.....where there's drinking, dancing, and flirting. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596293359i/29899621.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Leonard also seems to like Frances, and hangs around to talk to her whenever he gets the chance. Proximity and attraction lead to a relationship that ultimately results in a terrible accident, a police investigation, and a trial.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596293906i/29899655.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The author is adept at depicting emotions and the characters feelings of love, desire, worry, anger, despair, and so on seem authentic and true to life. That said, I didn't especially like many of the characters. Frances seems pushy and a little selfish, Lillian appears a bit manipulative and obtuse, and Leonard comes across as a bully and a lech. I felt some sympathy for Mrs. Wray, who lost her sons and husband and is bewildered by her daughter. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1596293359i/29899622.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And I was entertained by Lillian's large, boisterous family, who inject a needed touch of humor into the book.<br><br>To me the story was disturbing but this is a good book that's well-written and worth reading. Plus it contains subject matter that's good fodder for book clubs.<br><br>You can follow my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/">http://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5506423230207033068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer980935569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14532805711051893561">Now this was a BOOK.<br/><br/>I don't want to say much about this one. And I really don't think it's a good idea to go nosing about for information. Just wait patiently until it hits the shelves (or do what I did: stew about it and figure out a way to get a copy early and fast). Go into into this one as green as you can. Don't read any reviews (okay, maybe karen's: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/818412169?book_show_action=false&page=1'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> ). I've checked out the reviews, and there are a few gals who seem to be spoiler-buttoned-challenged </span>
  <span id="freeText14532805711051893561" style="display:none">Now this was a BOOK.<br /><br />I don't want to say much about this one. And I really don't think it's a good idea to go nosing about for information. Just wait patiently until it hits the shelves (or do what I did: stew about it and figure out a way to get a copy early and fast). Go into into this one as green as you can. Don't read any reviews (okay, maybe karen's: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/818412169?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> ). I've checked out the reviews, and there are a few gals who seem to be spoiler-buttoned-challenged and I would just hate for you to read something you shouldn't. Here are some general feelings on this book:<br /><br />Such a way with words! The characters were fantastic. Like real people. I loved Frances. She was perfect. This is how you set a scene. Was I there? I could swear I was there. It was almost like two books in one. There was a clear division (for me) between 'books'. I loved it all, but the 'first book' was just heaven (Delee, you'll feel the same way about the 'second book'). <br /><br />I'm so excited for all my gals who have this one to look forward to! It was allllll we knew it would be! I can't wait for your reviews. I'll be ready and willing to chat about this one, it won't soon leave me :)</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=sexuality">sexuality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer997864429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer21078329456708890">Pretty good as a time machine to London in the period of recovery after World War 1. A domestic drama plays out in the life of Frances and her mother that begins with taking on a young couple, the Barbers, as boarders. The economic pinch of the losses of a father and brothers in the war has them relinquishing an upper class life with servants and struggling to keep their house. The sense of invasion of their life to these “paying guests” is well done. The Barbers are of the “clerking class” who </span>
  <span id="freeText21078329456708890" style="display:none">Pretty good as a time machine to London in the period of recovery after World War 1. A domestic drama plays out in the life of Frances and her mother that begins with taking on a young couple, the Barbers, as boarders. The economic pinch of the losses of a father and brothers in the war has them relinquishing an upper class life with servants and struggling to keep their house. The sense of invasion of their life to these “paying guests” is well done. The Barbers are of the “clerking class” who dream of upward mobility, so the story of the evolution of their relationships with their new landlords represents a bit of a microcosm of the changing of British social structure in the 20s. However, any ferment of modernism has little impact on Frances’ ability to be open about her lesbianism. <br /><br />It’s hard to say anything more without spoiling the plot. I can say that much of the book excels in the atmospherics and hothouse claustrophobic feel of domestic life where secrets are guarded and only doled out in special circumstances of trust. There are open vistas with Frances’ love life and tragic complications that approach soap opera proportions. But unlike typical pot-boilers, no one emerges as a master of their fate and lessons learned remain obscure. I empathized with Frances, though she comes off as neither hero or villain, merely a relatively ordinary woman struggling to achieve self-fulfillment and challenged by tough moral decisions.<br /><br />For me this read was a step up from the only other book of hers I have read, the Gothic tale “The Night Watch”, though it was not as satisfying as other books I’ve rendered 4 stars for (i.e. a rounding up from 3.5). I look forward to trying her more well received books such as “Fingersmith” and “Tipping the Velvet.” <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="21078329456708890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1066685816" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1066685816" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1066685816?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1066685816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15649259410676003270">This is my first Sarah Waters and I was surprised to find it darker and creepier than her mainstream popularity led me to expect. I must have been adding my own embellishments as I read others’ reviews, creating my own reality. In a way, this is what Waters’ central character, Frances, does in this story about mores in 1920’s England and two women who try to find their way to love in secret.<br/><br/>Frances belongs to a genteel household which includes only herself and her mother now at the end of WWI wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15649259410676003270" style="display:none">This is my first Sarah Waters and I was surprised to find it darker and creepier than her mainstream popularity led me to expect. I must have been adding my own embellishments as I read others’ reviews, creating my own reality. In a way, this is what Waters’ central character, Frances, does in this story about mores in 1920’s England and two women who try to find their way to love in secret.<br /><br />Frances belongs to a genteel household which includes only herself and her mother now at the end of WWI which had taken off her brothers, and finished her father. To pay the bills, she invites two newly married boarders, "Len and Lil," to take up residence in the upstairs rooms. As Frances begins to hate pushy, overbearing, and snide Len, she simultaneously finds herself attracted to Lil. When Frances and Lil come together physically, I found myself disturbed—not so much by the idea of sex between two young women—as by the niggling sense of emotional coercion by Frances. As the story proceeded, that unease did not abate. And even Lil finally says it in the final chapters: "You always bully me, Frances."<br /> <br />The story is a long one, and we are drawn into the world of criminal courts and family relationships that complicate the budding romance between the two central characters. The sense of disaster hangs over one quite palpably as the relationship between the two starts to fray under the strain of their involvement in the criminal case before the courts. One might say that neither woman showed herself to advantage in the maelstrom of accusations and public interest, and therein lie the darkness: no matter that we readers knew of their role and could exonerate them, there was still something devious, in their hearts and in their actions, that made us dislike and distrust them nonetheless.<br /><br />This is a marvelous bit of writing that could make us so uneasy and feel the looming darkness in every scene. Much has been made of the sensuality with which the two women took to their bed, but it is that which made me uneasy. Lil was married but had never been properly “loved” and was therefore susceptible to someone who could bring her to orgasm. But sex, wondrous though it is, is not love after all. This is what I mean about “emotional coercion.” I think Frances knew this, and despite that knowledge, acted in her own self-interest and without the love she needed to make us more comfortable with her actions.<br /><br />Sarah Waters’ writing is beautifully crafted but Louise Welsh dark (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46025.The_Cutting_Room" title="The Cutting Room by Louise Welsh" rel="nofollow noopener">The Cutting Room</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/864915.The_Bullet_Trick" title="The Bullet Trick by Louise Welsh" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bullet Trick</a>) and Patricia Highsmith dark (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2247142.The_Talented_Mr__Ripley__Ripley___1_" title="The Talented Mr. Ripley (Ripley, #1) by Patricia Highsmith" rel="nofollow noopener">The Talented Mr. Ripley</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15677.Strangers_on_a_Train" title="Strangers on a Train by Patricia Highsmith" rel="nofollow noopener">Strangers on a Train</a>). Such a long book requires days of attention, and that darkness stays with one long after. Her roster of critical successes speaks to her talent.<br /><br />I listened to the audio of this title, generously provided by Penguin Random House Audio, and narrated with great skill and terrific accents by Juliet Stevenson. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15649259410676003270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1077605994" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1077605994" />
    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1077605994?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1077605994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5136717301218264709"><i>"They had no idea how decency, loyalty, courage, how it all shriveled away when one was frightened."</i><br/> <br/>So, days after finishing The Paying Guests I am still shaking my head in disbelief. Disbelief that a book can have the power to torment me and make me feel like it tied me to the rear bumper and dragged me backwards through vast wilderness of human emotions.<br/> <br/>The story is set in London of the 1920s complete with luscious descriptions of fashion and day-to-day details of the post-war life. Left in </span>
  <span id="freeText5136717301218264709" style="display:none"><i>"They had no idea how decency, loyalty, courage, how it all shriveled away when one was frightened."</i><br /> <br />So, days after finishing The Paying Guests I am still shaking my head in disbelief. Disbelief that a book can have the power to torment me and make me feel like it tied me to the rear bumper and dragged me backwards through vast wilderness of human emotions.<br /> <br />The story is set in London of the 1920s complete with luscious descriptions of fashion and day-to-day details of the post-war life. Left in difficult financial circumstances, Frances Wray and her mother have decided to take in lodgers to support their income. <br /> <br />What follows in the first half of this book is the slow but steady build up of tension which left me hanging on to every page with anticipation of when that tension would be released. If it would be released. <br />There is the tension between the characters. There is the issue of social tension, the contrast between the conventional and the modern, the question of roles. There is regret and the promise of making up for past mistakes. There is deceit and there is the test of courage. And...<br /><i> <br />"Didn't they almost have a duty to make one small brave thing happen at last?"</i><br /> <br />What The Paying Guests offers is a masterful web spun by Waters' meticulous detail and beautiful use of language. It's a psychological thriller in the tradition of Patricia Highsmith or Alfred Hitchcock, except that Waters' characters are more lifelike, likable, and that the full picture is not revealed until, ... well, not until, you have been taken on this rollercoaster ride, and have progressed through the trials that the characters face, and not until you have worried with them, lived with them, and loved with them. <br /> <br /><i>"But for now there was this, and it was enough. It was more than [I] could have hoped for."</i><br /> <br />Needless to say, Sarah Waters has just claimed her spot on my "favourites" shelf. <br />This was a difficult review, not just because there are so many aspects to this novel that I am unable to summarise but also because when reading The Paying Guests it pays off to know nothing about it. However, there is one passage which I would like to quote but really do not want to spoil the reading experience for anyone.<br /> <br />So only read on if you either have read the book or have no intention of reading it: <br /> <br /><i>
  <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">["They faced each other in the silence broken by the trickle of a faulty cistern, by the flutter of pigeons in the light-well. The room smelt of bleach and of sour wet mops. But Lilian looked back at her with eyes grown silvery with tears, and for a moment, the room, the trial, Leonard, the summer, their whole affair, it was as if none of it had yet happened. As if their love were to be done again, but done properly. As if they were back in Frances' bedroom the day after snakes and ladders, that imaginary stake just drawn from her heart. But across the moment there came from out in the hall the clanging of a bell, almost instantly followed by footsteps." <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</i><br /><br />Review first posted on BookLikes: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://brokentune.booklikes.com/post/1014612/the-paying-guests">http://brokentune.booklikes.com/post/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5136717301218264709" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Many have died, including the protagonist’s two brothers and her father. Those that returned from the war are disillusioned. Frances Wray and her mother are left bankrupt by their father who squandered away their money. They have dismissed the servants and are now taking in boarders. Frances does all the cleaning and cooking herself, while her mother is out, so that she will not have to watch her daughter stooping to that occupation.<br /><br />The guests who become “the paying guests” are a young couple of the clerk class, Mr. and Mrs. Barber (Leonard and Lilian.) Mr. Barber is talkative and makes Frances uncomfortable with his innuendos. Mrs. Barber hides herself away at first, but soon she and Frances develop a close friendship. As they grow closer, Frances divulges to Lilian that she had been in love with a woman, Christina, but was made to put an end to the relationship by her parents. In a time when London has been devastated by war, the family brought down by multiple deaths and financial ruin, certain societal norms are not to be challenged.<br /><br />The knowledge that Frances is a lesbian or had a lesbian lover seemingly creates a tension or barrier to their friendship. Lilian avoids Frances until the night of Lilian’s family party which she had invited Frances to many weeks prior in Mr. Barber’s stead as he had a supper to attend that evening.<br /><br />At the party, Mrs. Barber dances freely with several gentleman and even with Frances. After returning home, they find Leonard has been assaulted and is in the kitchen with a bloodied nose and face. Later that evening, Frances and Lilian return to the kitchen and embark on their steamy sultry love affair making love in the pantry. The love affair continues and their feelings continue to grow until Leonard is accidentally murdered which is ruled a homicide. This leads to a coverup, incredible tension, outing of other affairs, and the need for deep secrecy of their own love affair.<br /><br />This book is amazing on so many levels. The historical piece seems so spot on and well done. There was never a point where anything seemed even questionably out of the time period. I felt as if I were dwelling in London in the 1920s alongside these characters. The character building and tension that was created were so well done. I must admit I was getting antsy during the investigation and the trial that seemed to go on for so long, but that was the point. It keeps you on the edge of your seat. It keeps me questioning Lillian’s motives while still hoping the romance will last. This novel would make an excellent independent film with sexy enthralling characters. It would be amazing! It is an incredibly written book that I highly recommend to everyone. The one caveat is that it can seem to be going really slow at some points, which I didn't mind, but might not appeal to some.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3435759831175009328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1323238839" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1323238839" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5837456-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1372225128p2/5837456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1323238839?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/5837456-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22708132-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1323238839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13137583164762848041">This atmospheric novel is set in London, 1922. The country is still reeling after the First World War and, in Camberwell, widowed Mrs Wray and her spinster daughter, Frances, are just two women dealing with the aftermath. The house resounds with ghosts, including that of Mrs Wray’s sons, John Arthur and Noel, who died in the war. Frances, meanwhile, is full of resentment; much of it aimed at her dead father, who she felt bullied her brother’s into enlisting, before leaving her and her mother beh</span>
  <span id="freeText13137583164762848041" style="display:none">This atmospheric novel is set in London, 1922. The country is still reeling after the First World War and, in Camberwell, widowed Mrs Wray and her spinster daughter, Frances, are just two women dealing with the aftermath. The house resounds with ghosts, including that of Mrs Wray’s sons, John Arthur and Noel, who died in the war. Frances, meanwhile, is full of resentment; much of it aimed at her dead father, who she felt bullied her brother’s into enlisting, before leaving her and her mother behind in debt. Now, with no servants to help, Frances has given up plans for herself to spend her days doing the housework that her mother resents her having to do, yet chides her if it is not done properly. Resentment, an air of ‘making do’ and genteel poverty cloak their lives and, eventually, drastic means are called for. Frances decides that the only way of affording to live in their home is to take in lodgers – or, as they are called rather prosaically, ‘Paying Guests.’<br /><br />Into the lives of Frances and Mrs Wray come Leonard and Lilian Barber. They invade the space of the house and also enliven it. There is Len, with his jokey innuendos and cheerful air of activity; Lil with her bohemian inspired decorations, her mother and sisters, her conversation and feminine dresses. Although, at first, Frances finds it difficult to share the space of her home with others, eventually she is almost forced into contact with them. The couple have to go through the kitchen to the outside toilet and the upstairs landing houses Frances’ bedroom, as well as the Barber’s rooms.<br /><br />It is difficult to review this novel, without giving away the plot and I have no wish to do that. Frances is a complicated young woman, who feels she has been forced to give up her own wishes for that of her father and now her mother and the house. Her gradually building relationship with Lilian opens doors that she had firmly closed and results in a shocking event which forms the last third of the book.<br /> <br />Slow moving and emotional, the heart of this novel is, however, not really in the plot, but in the character of Frances herself. She is a product of her time – she believed in the suffragette movement and had dreams of a life and a career outside of the home. With the men of her generation killed in the war, she finds herself trapped in the home, embittered and lonely. Where so many authors throw plot and characters at you, Sarah Waters allows the storyline and characters to develop gradually. As you begin to know Frances and understand her, the plot unfolds before you – unstoppable and yet, so gradual that you almost feel it could be changed at any moment. Superb writing and literary fiction of the highest order make this a novel I will surely return to.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13137583164762848041" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9939148356976865774">This is a slow read and packed with lots of details of English domestic life so be warned. I plodded through semi-interested and occasionally riveted. The ending is a humdinger (don't you love that word) and so I am glad I stuck with it. I am a fan of the talented Welsh author Sarah Waters. If you haven't read "Fingersmith" or "The Little Stranger"...go pick up some copies.<br/> Her book is set in 1922 in Camberwell, South London, where a elderly widow and adult daughter live in a comfortable home. T</span>
  <span id="freeText9939148356976865774" style="display:none">This is a slow read and packed with lots of details of English domestic life so be warned. I plodded through semi-interested and occasionally riveted. The ending is a humdinger (don't you love that word) and so I am glad I stuck with it. I am a fan of the talented Welsh author Sarah Waters. If you haven't read "Fingersmith" or "The Little Stranger"...go pick up some copies.<br /> Her book is set in 1922 in Camberwell, South London, where a elderly widow and adult daughter live in a comfortable home. Times are tough and the servants are gone, so the daughter is part maid and cook for her mother. World War 1 has wipes out the young men in the area, including male family members, so everyone is in a state of mourning. They decide to take in lodgers to help bring in some much needed money and that is when the troubles start. A moral crisis begins and then the book comes to life.</span>

  <span id="freeText9222263586320060654" style="display:none">Groosly overwriten and cliched, the most glaring deficiency is a complete lack of firm, sympathetic editing. Key scenes, when they eventually arrive, are marred by repetition, bad use of adjectives and seriously poor punctuation.<br /><br />The main protagonists are completely two dimensional, particularly the ghastly, flaccid Lilian, and there is a passage in serious contention for the Bad Sex prize.<br /><br />The narrative is very obviously a close copy of 'A Pin To See The Peepshow', which is sad given Sarah Waters' former originality. <div id="review_1065109801" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1065109801" />
    <a title="Mona" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7020601-mona"><img alt="Mona" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392557851p2/7020601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1065109801?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mona" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mona" href="/user/show/7020601-mona">Mona</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780553544855" href="/book/show/23509185-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1065109801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5128092715912266364"><b>Admirable Historical Writing, but Too Unremittingly Bleak to be Enjoyable </b><br/><br/>This is a tough book to review.<br/><br/>I admired the author's skill.<br/><br/>The characters are believable, especially the very sympathetic Frances Wray, the main character.<br/><br/>Her accuracy of time and place is excellent. So is the muted tone of the prose.<br/><br/>And Juliet Stevenson did a nearly superhuman job reading the audio. This cannot have been the easiest material to read. <br/><br/>But....<br/><br/>The book is so bleak (although the ending is more hopeful than m</span>
  <span id="freeText5128092715912266364" style="display:none"><b>Admirable Historical Writing, but Too Unremittingly Bleak to be Enjoyable </b><br /><br />This is a tough book to review.<br /><br />I admired the author's skill.<br /><br />The characters are believable, especially the very sympathetic Frances Wray, the main character.<br /><br />Her accuracy of time and place is excellent. So is the muted tone of the prose.<br /><br />And Juliet Stevenson did a nearly superhuman job reading the audio. This cannot have been the easiest material to read. <br /><br />But....<br /><br />The book is so bleak (although the ending is more hopeful than much of what proceeds it), that it wasn't exactly a fun read. I can't call it completely tragic, because of the ending. Still, many tragic things occur. There's little humor to relieve the darkness, although there are occasional funny moments here and there. <br /><br />The pace is slow (almost sleepy, in keeping with the time and place), especially in the first half of the book. Midway through the book, an event occurs which alters everything. After that, it's still slow going, but there's a lot more tension. <br /><br />Frances Wray, a 26 year old woman, and her aging mother are impoverished British aristocrats living in the upscale Champion Hill section of Camberwell in 1922 London. They live in a large old (and once fine) house. They are forced to take in lodgers ("paying guests" in the polite lingo of the time) to make ends meet. They are in this situation because Frances' father was apparently a careless man who made some bad investments, so when he died they were left with little except the house.<br /><br />The families' two sons were killed in World War I. So this is a time when women were running things in Britain because so many of the men died in the war. <br /><br />The capable Frances does nearly all of the household chores, as the Wrays can no longer afford servants.<br /><br />The Wrays choose a married couple, Lilian and Leonard Barber, as lodgers.<br /><br />I won't say more about the story (to avoid spoilers) except that the Barbers bring the Wrays more than just a rent check.<br /><br />Frances, the main character, is entirely believable and sympathetic. We feel for her ups and downs (and there are many).<br /><br />I found this novel vaguely reminiscent (at times) of other British authors. There was a whiff of Virginia Woolf (in the glimpses of inner sadness and loneliness), of Harold Pinter (in the vague and unstated menace) and of American writer Connie Willis who writes about Britain (in the lack of communication between clueless characters). Possibly these are themes that British authors favor.<br /><br />I can live with a book that has a slow pace, although I found this book a bit too long. But the grimness of Frances' world started to really get me down. I suppose in a way that's a testament to the author's ability to create a character with whom readers can empathize. Also, everyone is so trapped in the conventions and prejudices of Britain in 1922. Certainly those conventions make Frances' life very difficult.<br /><br />BTW, the women are (mostly) sympathetic characters, although Lilian Barber is a mixed bag. They tell many lies, but somehow we don't hold it against them. But the male characters are nearly uniformly predatory, unsympathetic louts. Is this because as the refrain in the book goes "the best of them died in the War?" Or does it reflect the author's bias? Hard to say for certain.<br /><br />I prefer a read that, while it may be tragic, has some leavening of wit or lightness here and there. <br /><br />So that's why I gave this only three stars, although Sarah Waters is clearly an excellent writer of historical fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5128092715912266364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_938807510" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/938807510" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/938807510?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780349004600" href="/book/show/24568234-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=read-more-women">read-more-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=sarah-waters">sarah-waters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer938807510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer994639271473162028"><br/>It is year 1922, the dust after the turmoil of war hardly fell, the higher social classes have lost their fortunes and former status and are forced to live in new circumstances. Such fate shares 26-year-old Frances Wray who lives with her mother in decaying house in the suburbs of London. Her brothers died at the front and father died leaving family in debts. Frances, formerly feisty activist and suffragist all at once has a lot on her plate. She’s a maid and gardener, cleaning lady and nurse. H</span>
  <span id="freeText994639271473162028" style="display:none"><br />It is year 1922, the dust after the turmoil of war hardly fell, the higher social classes have lost their fortunes and former status and are forced to live in new circumstances. Such fate shares 26-year-old Frances Wray who lives with her mother in decaying house in the suburbs of London. Her brothers died at the front and father died leaving family in debts. Frances, formerly feisty activist and suffragist all at once has a lot on her plate. She’s a maid and gardener, cleaning lady and nurse. Her days go on scrubbing floors, dusting, cleaning, cooking and caring about a bit neurotic and overbearing mother. And horror of horrors !, in mother’s eyes and sometimes in her own too she is at risk of spinsterhood.<br /><br />To make ends meet Frances has to rent rooms and take lodgers, young couple, Lily and Leonard Barber, <i> two walking shillings </i>. And from that moment nothing would be the same. This is a starting point for <i> Paying guests </i>.<br /> <br />Writing, set in victorian England <i> Fingersmith </i> Sarah Waters recklessly broke into literary Parnassus. And set herself the bar really high. Her latest novel doesn't dissapoint, it's precisely constructed tale that little by little transforms from the novel of manners into romance, from romance into thriller. To finally become a deep psychological drama. <br /><br />The story unfolds rather slowly, we get an accurate descriptions of post-war England, this world when men having returned from the war could not quite pull themselves together while women slowly began to gain more and more space and social consciousness. I found both psychological portraits of the protagonists and their life choices, courage and moral dilemmas very well written.<br /><br />At some point however, lulled a bit with some of these mundane aspects, all of a sudden we find themselves in the middle of a forbidden romance. I'm not going to reveal more to not spoil anyone’s reading. I can recommend this one and though this is not <i> Fingersmith </i> it’s still really good and engrossing piece of writing.<br /><br />3,5/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="994639271473162028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_796694899" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/796694899" />
    <a title="Hanneke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17944903-hanneke"><img alt="Hanneke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546964664p2/17944903.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/796694899?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hanneke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hanneke" href="/user/show/17944903-hanneke">Hanneke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780349004365" href="/book/show/18485452-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer796694899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1556047287874919781">I have mixed feelings about this book. I thought it was very boring for too long as nothing really happens in the first 250 pages. True, we get a very atmospheric description of the 1920's, the class system, the devastating result of WW-I on family life and on British society as a whole. However, 100 pages would have been quite enough to present the setting and tell us a silly romance story with lots of secret panting and sighing and groping in the pantry. By page 250 I was tempted to toss the b</span>
  <span id="freeText1556047287874919781" style="display:none">I have mixed feelings about this book. I thought it was very boring for too long as nothing really happens in the first 250 pages. True, we get a very atmospheric description of the 1920's, the class system, the devastating result of WW-I on family life and on British society as a whole. However, 100 pages would have been quite enough to present the setting and tell us a silly romance story with lots of secret panting and sighing and groping in the pantry. By page 250 I was tempted to toss the book aside, but didn't as I could not imagine that Sarah Waters was boring us to death. And, sure enough, the story then explodes and becomes interesting, albeit never becomes really exciting. Frances did not invoke any sympathy with me and I did not like it how she pursued Lily, throwing her higher class in as an extra weight. I regret that the wit which made books like Fingersmith and Tipping the Velvet so enjoyable is totally lacking in this book. Paul expresses it very well in his review. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1556047287874919781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2351859219" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2351859219" />
    <a title="Lotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25309949-lotte"><img alt="Lotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408555448p2/25309949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2351859219?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lotte" href="/user/show/25309949-lotte">Lotte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780349004600" href="/book/show/24568234-the-paying-guests">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=ge-suspense-noir">ge-suspense-noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=a-fiction">a-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=t-lgbtqia">t-lgbtqia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=t-women-misbehaving">t-women-misbehaving</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=ge-historical-fiction">ge-historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=2019-audiobook">2019-audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25309949-lotte?shelf=ge-historical-mystery">ge-historical-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2351859219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18279693069983581792"><b>3.75/5.</b> There are so, so many things I love about Sarah Waters' writing. Her prose is stunning – it's (for lack of a better term) literary, but still easy to get into, and makes her books simultaneously challenging and comforting, which I think is a truly unique feature. Her writing also always manages to completely envelop me in the atmosphere of her novels and she's such an expert at inserting subtle, but poignant commentary on the historical periods her novels are set in. Plus, her characters</span>
  <span id="freeText18279693069983581792" style="display:none"><b>3.75/5.</b> There are so, so many things I love about Sarah Waters' writing. Her prose is stunning – it's (for lack of a better term) literary, but still easy to get into, and makes her books simultaneously challenging and comforting, which I think is a truly unique feature. Her writing also always manages to completely envelop me in the atmosphere of her novels and she's such an expert at inserting subtle, but poignant commentary on the historical periods her novels are set in. Plus, her characters feel like real human beings and while I don't always necessarily like them, they're written with such care and depth that I can always understand and feel for them.<br />That being said, I ended up giving <i>The Paying Guests</i> "only" 3.75 stars overall. In trying to describe what bothered me, I'm basically just repeating what I said <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1767851889" rel="nofollow noopener">in my review</a> of her other novel, <i>The Little Stranger</i> - it's <i>way too long</i>. I definitely agree with what Abbie mentions <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2549670372" rel="nofollow noopener">in her review</a> about Sarah Waters needing a harsher editor. Especially in the latter half of the novel the plot loses itself in too many unnecessary repetitions and I didn't feel the slow-burning prickle that made the first half so enjoyable (which, thinking about it now, might have been her point as it fits the direction the plot is taking, but still). Basically, as with <i>The Little Stranger</i>, this could've easily been a 5-star read for me had it been 100-150 pages shorter. <br />I have now read three of Sarah Waters' six books and I'm incredibly excited to read the remaining three, especially <i>Fingersmith</i>, which seems to be her most popular one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="18279693069983581792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('P4G4944oaRtQG09xwJtAqob6n1IWUj+ps7Nr1AtbPTu4+jHbOQhIdiPEjg5F1fQFhIsrlWehj98UpMg0ltWuRQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

The story begins with the death of the community\'s esteemed rabbi, which sets in motion plans for a memorial service and the search for a replacement. The rabbi\'s nephew and likely successor, Dovid, calls his cousin Ronit in New York to tell her that her father has died. Ronit, who left the community long ago to build a life for herself as a career woman, returns home when she hears the news, and her reappearance exposes tears in the fabric of the community.\n\nSteeped in Jewish philosophy and teachings, Disobedience is a perceptive and thoughtful exploration of the laws and practices that have governed Judaism for centuries, and continue to hold sway today. Throughout the novel, Alderman retells stories from the Torah -- Judaism\'s fundamental source -- and the interplay between these tales and the struggles of the novel\'s unique characters wields           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6708143517046219531"><i>Sarah Waters</i> is a British novelist. She is best known for her first novel, <i>Tipping the Velvet</i>, as well the novels that followed, including <i>Affinity</i>, <i>Fingersmith</i>, and <i>The Night Watch</i>.<br/><br/>Waters attended university, earning degrees in English literature. Before writing novels Waters worked as an academic, earning a doctorate and teaching. Waters went directly from her doctoral thesis to her first novel.</span>
  <span id="freeText6708143517046219531" style="display:none"><i>Sarah Waters</i> is a British novelist. She is best known for her first novel, <i>Tipping the Velvet</i>, as well the novels that followed, including <i>Affinity</i>, <i>Fingersmith</i>, and <i>The Night Watch</i>.<br /><br />Waters attended university, earning degrees in English literature. Before writing novels Waters worked as an academic, earning a doctorate and teaching. Waters went directly from her doctoral thesis to her first novel. It was during the process of writing her thesis that she thought she would write a novel; she began as soon as the thesis was complete.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6708143517046219531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
